        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202304587')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8c846a08fca74755bb8c43b0a548db56">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'af3f6cc5-8ea2-4138-9b3a-edda1f2347ea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '6E7983EAE7ED559FB9EFD26B2D91D3F7';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d73807934" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received tremendous attention owing to their low cost, high working voltages, and energy density. However, the design and development of highly efficient SIBs represent a great challenge. Here, a unique and reliable approach is reported to prepare carbon nitride (CN) hybridized with nickel iron sulfide (NFCN) using simple reaction between Ni–Fe layered double hydroxide and dithiooxamide. The characterization results demonstrate that the hybridization with optimal amount of CN induces local distortion in the crystal structure of the hybrid, which would benefit SIB performance. Systematic electrochemical studies with a half-cell configuration show that the present hybrid structure exhibits a promising reversible specific capacity of 348 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with good rate capability. Simulation result reveals that the iron atoms in nickel iron sulfide act as a primary active site to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions. At last, with a full cell configuration using NFCN and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F as the anode and cathode, respectively, the specific capacity appears to be ≈95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> condition. This excellent performance of these hybrids can be attributed to the synergistic effect of the incorporated CN species and the high conductivity of nickel–iron sulfide.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304587-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304587-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) offer an alternative solution to the lithium-ion battery (LIB) system due to the high natural abundance and environmental-friendliness of sodium, appropriate redox potential, and similar operating principles to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0001_R_d73807926e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202304587-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0004_R_d73807926e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Rev. B">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, no alloying reaction between sodium and aluminum can trigger a total replacement of copper foil on the anode electrode, which gives rise to a substantial reduction of “dead weight” and concomitant enhancement of energy density as well as lower manufacturing cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0005_R_d73807926e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the larger size/mass of sodium ions diminishes the SIB performance due to the sluggish Na-ion diffusion kinetics, uncontrollable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer and large volumetric changes in the anode materials during sodiation and desodiation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0006_R_d73807926e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, addressing these issues by developing advanced electrode materials with high stability and rate performance is highly critical for the commercial feasibility of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Metal sulfides have shown great potential as anode materials for the SIB system through their high theoretical capacity based on the conversion reaction, higher electrical conductivity than metal oxide type, moderate reaction platform, and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0004_R_d73807926e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Rev. B">4</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0006_R_d73807926e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">6</a>-<a href="#smll202304587-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0008_R_d73807926e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Lett.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, metal sulfides display inferior SIB performance to their expected theoretical capacities due to large volumetric change with uncontrollable reactive intermediate during the conversion reaction, causing additional side reactions which not only distort the crystal structure but also consume the Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This leads to a rapid capacity loss with unsatisfactory performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0009_R_d73807926e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0010_R_d73807926e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to mitigate this issue, various forms of support systems have been explored to hybridize with metal sulfides effectively. Specifically, carbon nitrides (CNs) with varying stoichiometric ratios of C and N atoms have gained significant attention due to their remarkable capacity to facilitate efficient hybridization. Furthermore, their systematic control over physicochemical properties makes them versatile candidates for various energy-related applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0011_R_d73807926e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Batteries Supercaps">11</a>-<a href="#smll202304587-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0019_R_d73807926e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides the role of a support, CN can attract alkali metal ions through a lone pair of N atoms, which also can contribute to capacity generation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0011_R_d73807926e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Batteries Supercaps">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the studies of the fabrication of novel CN-based hybrid systems with high nitrogen contents and metal sulfides have been quite limited.</p>
         
         <p>In this investigation, we have prepared a hybrid structure of nickel iron sulfide and carbon nitride (NFCN) using a Ni-Fe layered double hydroxide (LDH) as a precursor for Ni and Fe elements and dithiooxamide (DTO) as the single molecular source for CN and S (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0001">1A</a>). Constructing a bimetallic phase in the metal sulfide could improve the electrochemical performance via richer redox reactions, more active sites and faster reaction kinetics due to the synergistic effect of the multiphase boundaries of crystalline domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0008_R_d73807926e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Lett.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0020_R_d73807926e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a>-<a href="#smll202304587-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0022_R_d73807926e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Mater. Chem. Front.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the strong hybridization with the CN would not only stabilize the pulverization effect of the metal sulfide during cycling but also facilitate the charge transfer at the heterointerface. As such, we have demonstrated the beneficial effect of the CN system to promote not only the SIB performance with half-cell and full cell configurations but also the charge transfer property and surface contribution of bimetallic sulfide.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304587-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304587-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The XRD patterns indicate successful formation of pentlandite nickel iron sulfide with <i>Fm</i>-3<i>m</i> space group (Figure <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0001">1B</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0023_R_d73807926e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Cryst. Growth">23</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0024_R_d73807926e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pentlandite phase is primarily observed for NFCN2 and NFCN3. On the other hand, NFCN1 with less amount of CN exhibits additional intense XRD peaks other than the pentlandite, which corresponds to Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub> phase, suggesting uneven crystal formation that is separated into two different phases. This could be attributed to lacking the anchoring sites present on the CN template, which stabilizes and crystallizes the bimetallic sulfide particles. According to the Scherrer equation, primary particle sizes of nickel iron sulfide were estimated to be 23–27&nbsp;nm for all the NFCN. The FESEM images show flexible nanosheet morphologies with a low thickness for NFCN2-3 hybrids, whereas NFCN1 appears a moderately aggregated feature due to insufficient CN content to prevent the agglomeration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0001">1C</a>, the HRTEM analysis cross-confirms the flexible nanosheet morphology for the NFCN2 hybrid. Elemental energy-dispersive x-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping of the NFCN hybrid presents all the C, N, S, Ni, and Fe atoms to be uniformly distributed, suggesting homogeneous hybridization between CN and nickel iron sulfide crystallites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0001">1D</a>). According to N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption analyses (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the surface areas are estimated to be 8.0, 9.7, and 23.0 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for NFCN1-3, underscoring increase in surface area as the CN content increases. In the micro-Raman results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), all the NFCN hybrids exhibit D and G bands at ≈1350 and ≈1600 cm⁻¹, respectively, with minimal variation in the intensity ratios of these bands across the hybrids. The chemical compositions of the NFCN hybrids are shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202304587-tbl-0001">1</a>. All the hybrids exhibit a dominant portion of the CN relative to the Ni and Fe contents. Furthermore, the N contents in the NFCN hybrids appear to increase in inverse proportion to the Ni and Fe species, which might be due to the presence of hydroxyl groups in the LDH precursor that consumes N elements while calcination.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/79d7eb1a-71aa-4a9e-9ca4-8fc24ddd26f8/smll202304587-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/79d7eb1a-71aa-4a9e-9ca4-8fc24ddd26f8/smll202304587-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/698580a5-7900-4e38-b2e8-c82d98770576/smll202304587-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/79d7eb1a-71aa-4a9e-9ca4-8fc24ddd26f8/smll202304587-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The NEXAFS analyses were performed to investigate the nature of chemical bonding of the NFCN hybrids. According to C K-edge spectra, four different characteristic features (peaks C<sub>1</sub>–C<sub>4</sub>) are detected for the NFCN hybrids at 285, 287.3, 288.3, and 290&nbsp;eV which correspond to C 1s → π<sub>*</sub> transitions of C═C, C─N, aromatic C─N─C, and C═O bonding, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4A</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0025_R_d73807926e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N K-edge spectra show two peaks of N<sub>1</sub>, N<sub>2,</sub> and N<sub>3</sub> at 398.0, 399.3, and 401.3&nbsp;eV for all the NFCN hybrids which correspond to the N 1s → π<sub>*</sub> transitions of aromatic C─N─C (pyridinic-like N), pyrrolic N, and graphitic N, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4B</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0026_R_d73807926e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> S L-edge spectra display typical characteristics for metal sulfide crystallite (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4C</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0027_R_d73807926e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> C–S–C bonding below 170&nbsp;eV is not observed, which reflects negligible S substitution into the CN lattice. The chemical structures of Ni and Fe were investigated through XPS analyses which indicates Ni L<sub>3,2</sub>-edge spectra exhibit two main peaks at ≈855 and ≈873&nbsp;eV, which are assigned as 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0028_R_d73807926e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Phys. Rev. B">28</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0029_R_d73807926e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Mater. Today Phys.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For Fe L<sub>3,2</sub>-edge spectra, the peak are deconvoluted to be mixed divalent and trivalent oxidation states (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0028_R_d73807926e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Phys. Rev. B">28</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0030_R_d73807926e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The XAS measurements were conducted to understand local crystal structure around Fe and Ni atoms of the NFCN hybrids. The Fe K-edge XANES spectra reveals that all the NFCN hybrids possess mixed oxidation states of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0002">2A</a>). As different from iron oxide references, all the hybrid materials appear low pre-edge features which underscores small portion of Fe atoms in tetrahedral geometry as well as major in octahedral symmetry. The pre-edge feature can be also observed for Ni K-edge spectra of the NFCN hybrids, confirming that the Ni atoms are coordinating in both tetrahedral and octahedral symmetries (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0002">2B</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0031_R_d73807926e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Geol.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further investigation on the local crystal structure of the present materials was carried out via EXAFS analysis. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0002">2C,D</a> illustrates the FT data of the <i>k</i><sup>3</sup>-weighted Fe K-edge and Ni K-edge EXAFS oscillations of NFCN hybrids, respectively. The first FT peaks in the Fe K-edge and Ni K-edge represent Fe─S and Ni─S bonds, respectively. While negligible change is observed for the Fe─S bond, the NFCN2 exhibits a distinct decrease of FT intensity of the Ni─S bond in stark contrast to NFCN1 and NFCN3. Given the fact that the FT intensity is inverse proportional to local structural disorder,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0032_R_d73807926e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this observation indicates that the hybridization with CN in the NFCN2 induces nickel iron sulfide structure with enhanced local structure disorder around Ni atom. To quantitively estimate the local structural parameters for the present hybrids, the fitting analysis was conducted for the present EXAFS spectra using pentlandite nickel iron sulfide with <i>Fm</i>-3<i>m</i> space group. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the NFCN2 shows larger Debye-Waller factor (<i>σ</i><sup>2</sup>) for Ni–S bond than NFCN1 and NFCN3. Since the <i>σ</i><sup>2</sup> value is proportional to structural disorder,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0032_R_d73807926e966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the fitting results clearly demonstrate increase of structural disorder for NFCN2 hybrid. The observed localized distortion around the Ni atom in the NFCN2 hybrid may be attributed to the well-defined heterointerface formed between nickel iron sulfide and carbon nitride. This heterointerface is likely instrumental in promoting improved battery performance by facilitating charge transfer. The observed structural disorder could beneficially influence on SIB performance of the NFCN2 as it is well-known that the presence of high structural disorder is preferable for SIB performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0033_R_d73807926e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, for further clarification, additional EXAFS measurements, along with control experiments, should be undertaken to determine the presence of the actual bonds of Ni–C or Ni–N.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09c514fe-53c1-4440-ac11-af6b647eae36/smll202304587-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/09c514fe-53c1-4440-ac11-af6b647eae36/smll202304587-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc053880-3645-426d-be78-12befe770175/smll202304587-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09c514fe-53c1-4440-ac11-af6b647eae36/smll202304587-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) Fe K-edge and B) Ni K-edge XANES spectra of NFCN hybrids. FTs of C) Fe K-edge and D) Ni K-edge EXAFS data of NFCN hybrids.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The evolution of electrochemical performance of the NFCN hybrids was confirmed by measuring a cycling behavior toward Na<sup>+</sup> ions at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0003">3A</a>). At the initial cycling, initial discharge capacities were detected to be 462, 475, and 423 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NFCN1, NFCN2 and NFCN3, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 60–75% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The contribution of the primary redox reaction to battery performance was investigated through cyclic voltammetry (CV) analyses. In the CV curves of the initial three cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), all the hybrid materials exhibit a pronounced cathodic peak at ≈0.7&nbsp;V, which correlates with the SEI formation. Notably, this peak diminishes significantly starting from the second CV cycle, indicating the rapid stabilization of the SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0034_R_d73807926e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the NFCN hybrids show intense peaks at ≈1.65&nbsp;V (anodic direction) and ≈0.85&nbsp;V (cathodic direction), which correspond to a conversion reaction of nickel sulfide species (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0003">3B</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0035_R_d73807926e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nanoscale">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are additional peaks observed at ≈1.45&nbsp;V (anodic direction) and ≈0.3–0.4&nbsp;V (cathodic direction) that coincide with a redox pair of the conversion reaction of iron sulfide species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0011_R_d73807926e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Batteries Supercaps">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, a notably broad cathodic peak emerges at ≈1.2&nbsp;V that aligns with the sodiation process of the iron sulfide phase although it may be negligible for NFCN1 and NFCN3 due to very intense peak at ≈0.85&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0036_R_d73807926e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NFCN2 also exhibits an anodic peak at ≈0.18&nbsp;V, which may be attributed to the desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions on the surface of the CN.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0037_R_d73807926e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that the corresponding cathodic peak might be interfered by other intense CV peaks.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea218779-eb30-4805-a8d7-a20f79fb382e/smll202304587-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea218779-eb30-4805-a8d7-a20f79fb382e/smll202304587-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/386870d8-610b-43a1-b584-5be48a532581/smll202304587-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea218779-eb30-4805-a8d7-a20f79fb382e/smll202304587-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Half-cell configuration: A) Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and subsequent rate capability at 0.05–0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and B) CV curves for the hybrids NFCN1 (red), NFCN2 (blue) and NFCN3 (yellow). Nyquist plots for the hybrids C) NFCN1, D) NFCN2 and E) NFCN3 with a comparison of before (at OCV) and after SIB cycling.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>During cycling, the discharge capacity of NFCN1 appears to slightly degrade from 369 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (2<sup>nd</sup> cycle) to 319 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (100<sup>th</sup> cycle), as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4A,B</a> (Supporting Information). On the contrary, the specific capacities of the NFCN2-3 samples are barely degraded, which underscores a beneficial effect of the CN on enhancing the durability of nickel–iron sulfide as well as suppressing the formation of impurity phase. Upon 100 cycles, the discharge capacities were detected to be 319, 348 and 339 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for NFCN1, NFCN2 and NFCN3, respectively, confirming that the NFCN2 is the optimized material with the highest performance. Further cycling tests stresses the high-rate capability of the NFCN2 that maintains the specific capacity over 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at the higher current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Also, the discharge capacity at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> is fully recovered even after passing through the higher current density, which suggests good reversibility for NFCN2. However, the SEM image obtained after 100 cycles reveals significant agglomeration, with only a few remaining morphological features of NSs for NFCN2, which underscores a substantial change in morphology during battery cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The observed battery performance for the NFCN hybrid appears to outweigh that for iron sulfide-CN hybrid (FCN). The beneficial effect of bimetallic sulfide over single metallic species stands out as increasing the current densities. The FCN exhibits obvious capacity fading as increasing the current densities, which is in stark contrast to the NFCN2 that shows quite constant capacities with current densities. Also, both CN-free nickel iron sulfide and nickel sulfide display inferior battery performance to the NFCN hybrid, underscoring the advantage of CN hybridization in enhancing battery performance (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10 and S11</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The charge transfer property of the NFCN hybrids is confirmed through electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analyses. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0003">3C–E</a>, all the EIS spectra measured at OCV (before cycling) possess a well-defined semicircle, which is associated with the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>). NFCN1 possesses a larger semicircle relative to the NFCN2-3 hybrids, underscoring a larger <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> property for NFCN1 than that of the others. Upon battery cycling, the EIS spectra feature with two different semicircles at high and mid frequency ranges, which can be assigned to the resistances from the passivating SEI layer and charge transfer behavior. To quantify the resistance parameters, further simulations using equivalent circuits including solution resistance (<i>R</i><sub>Sol</sub>), SEI resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> were carried out (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12 and S13</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The simulation confirms the obvious decrease in the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> value from NFCN1 to NFCN3 after battery cycling, which suggests the hybridized CN template could beneficially affect the SEI formation to be more permeable toward Na<sup>+</sup> ions. It should be noted that the NFCN1-2 hybrids could be properly simulated by a single <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> parameter, which would be primarily affected by nickel iron sulfide with negligible contribution of the CN species on capacity generation. In other words, CN is likely to mainly act as a hybridization template in the NFCN1-2 hybrids. On the other hand, a Nyquist circuit with two <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> parameters induced a proper fitting for NFCN3, which would infer two different domains of nickel iron sulfide and CN influencing on the electrochemical behavior of NFCN3.</p>
         
         <p>The CVs with different scan rates of 0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> were carried out to investigate the dominant electrochemical behavior toward Na<sup>+</sup> storage for the present NFCN hybrids (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0004">4A–C</a>). The <i>b</i>-value can be estimated based on the equation I = α<i>ν<sup>b</sup></i> (I = measured current, <i>ν</i> = sweep rate and α- and <i>b</i>-values = variable parameters). Theoretically, the electrochemical behavior is governed by the slow semi-infinite diffusion-controlled process that would lead to a <i>b</i>-value of 0.5, whereas a (pseudo)capacitive process would lead to a <i>b</i>-value of 1.0.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0038_R_d73807926e1175" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Phys. Chem. C">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>b</i>-values of NFCN hybrids are obtained via plotting of the logarithmic peak currents versus the logarithmic scan rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0004">4D–F</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0038_R_d73807926e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Phys. Chem. C">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the <i>b</i>-value estimation, we chose two redox pairs (Ox<sub>1</sub>/Red<sub>1</sub> and Ox<sub>2</sub>/Red<sub>2</sub>) that appear to mainly contribute on the battery performance. The redox 1 and 2 are attributed to the conversion reactions of iron sulfides and nickel sulfides, respectively. The <i>b</i>-values of the redox pairs for the NFCN1-3 hybrids lie within 0.8–0.9, implying a substantial contribution of pseudocapacitive behavior on the conversion reaction of these bimetallic sulfides. This can be also confirmed by quantifying the contributions of these two processes to the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior via calculation of the variables <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> based on the equation <i>i</i>(V)/ν<sup>1/2</sup> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> indicate the contributions of (pseudo)capacitive and diffusion-controlled behaviors, respectively. The (pseudo)capacitive distribution data for NFCN hybrids are depicted as the colored area in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14A–C</a> (Supporting Information). As shown in the quantitative contributions as per scan rates of 0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14D–F</a>, Supporting Information), the (pseudo)capacitive contribution to NFCN1 is estimated to be slightly less than NFCN2 and NFCN3, which can be ascribed to a lack of the CN species in the chemical composition of NFCN1. The CN nanosheet plays a pivotal role in the prevention of agglomeration of the bimetallic sulfide nanoparticles, which can secure plenty of surface-active sites, improving the (pseudo)capacitive contribution in the overall battery performance. On the other hand, NFCN1 fails to acquire sufficient surface-active sites because of severe agglomeration of the metallic species, as observed by SEM analysis.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/635efc12-d60f-4d8b-8d86-6ff30ba01bc1/smll202304587-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/635efc12-d60f-4d8b-8d86-6ff30ba01bc1/smll202304587-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e1f2dfa-7c59-48e8-93b4-e55fb853d9f8/smll202304587-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/635efc12-d60f-4d8b-8d86-6ff30ba01bc1/smll202304587-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A–C) CVs within a scan rate of 0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and D–F) graphs plotting the logarithmic peak currents versus the logarithmic scan rate as per the observed redox behaviors for the hybrids A,D) NFCN1, B,E) NFCN2 and C,F) NFCN3. In the A–C, red, orange, yellow, green, blue and dark blue represent the scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8 and 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In the D–F, the figure in round brackets represent the estimated <i>b</i>-values for the corresponding redox behaviors.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To explore the active site of the NFCN hybrid for the SIB performance, simulations were conducted after optimizing the NFCN structure. The optimized structure of the CN–nickel iron sulfide (111) surface is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). Initially, the bottom layer of (111) surface was kept at a distance of 2.5 Å from the CN layer, which increased to 3.42 Å after the hybrid structure optimization indicating the Van-der-Waals type interaction between CN and (111) surface layers. In order to assess the adsorption of Na atom on the NFCN hybrid structure (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0005">5</a>), we considered three Top-sites, viz. Fe, Ni, and S and the adsorption energy is calculated using Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202304587-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)s

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304587-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e513a28d-92e7-407d-b23e-8ca7dedacbd1/smll202304587-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304587:smll202304587-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304587-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>E</mi>
                              <mi>ads</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>E</mi>
                              <mi>AB</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>E</mi>
                              <mi>A</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>E</mi>
                              <mi>B</mi>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{E}_{{\mathrm{ads}}} = \ {E}_{{\mathrm{AB}}} - \ {E}_A - \ {E}_B\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>E</i><sub>AB</sub> represents the total energy of NFCN hybrid-Na system, <i>E</i><sub>A</sub>, denotes the energy of NFCN hybrid and <i>E</i><sub>B</sub> is the energy of Na atom. The <i>E</i><sub>B</sub> is obtained by calculating the total energy of Na bulk in BCC crystal structure. The calculated adsorption energy of Na is shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202304587-tbl-0002">2</a>. As shown in the Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202304587-tbl-0002">2</a>, the Na atom shows highest adsorption energy when placed on top of the Fe atom and minimum when Na atom is placed on top of S atom. The Na adsorption energy on the NFCN hybrid follows the order as <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>Fe &gt; <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>Ni &gt; <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>S. Thus, Fe top-site is the most favorable site followed by Ni and S top-sites, though the difference in the adsorption energy over Ni and S top-site is only 20 meV.</div>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="smll202304587-tbl-0002">
            
            
            
            
            <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  2.
                    </span>The calculated adsorption energy of Na atom over the top of the Fe/Ni/S atoms in the NFCN hybrid and Bader charge transfer from Na atom to the (111) surface of the hybrid</header>
            
            
            
            <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">
               <table class="table article-section__table">
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  <thead>
                     
                     <tr>

                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Ni</th>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">S</th>
                        </tr>
                     </thead>
                  
                  <tbody>
                     
                     <tr>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2c582a32-f3f3-4782-b986-6d98ac7f33b4/smll202304587-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2c582a32-f3f3-4782-b986-6d98ac7f33b4/smll202304587-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8ad3232a-ad93-4a41-8135-b5f69f524640/smll202304587-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2c582a32-f3f3-4782-b986-6d98ac7f33b4/smll202304587-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Side view (upper row) and top view (bottom row) of the optimized structure of Na over top of the A) Fe, B) Ni, and C) S atom on the NFCN (111) surface heterostructure. Na atom is represented by the red ball.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The calculated Bader charge transfer from Na atom to the heterostructure is also given in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202304587-tbl-0002">2</a>. We see that the charge transfer is maximum when Na atom is at the Fe top-site followed by S and Ni top-sites. The results are consistent with the adsorption energy as Fe top-site is found to show highest adsorption energy. Comparing the Bader charge transfer in cases of Ni and S as top-sites, we see opposite trend between charge transfer and adsorption energy. This behavior can be related to the differential charge density<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0039_R_d73807926e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0040_R_d73807926e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - AIP Adv.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (∆<i>ρ</i>) plots as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0006">6</a> and is calculated as

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304587-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bf8b1195-b8a8-40f5-8eae-e0ec357cfda2/smll202304587-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304587:smll202304587-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304587-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                           <mi>ρ</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>ρ</mi>
                              <mi>AB</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>ρ</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>ρ</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\Delta \rho = \Delta {\rho }_{{\mathrm{AB}}} - \Delta {\rho }_{\mathrm{A}} - \Delta {\rho }_{\mathrm{B}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where, the subscripts (AB, A and B) carry the same meaning as defined above. The yellow isosurface represents the negative region (charge depletion), while the cyan isosurface shows the positive region (charge accumulation).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0040_R_d73807926e1500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - AIP Adv.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We see that the charge is more diffused when Na atom is placed on the top of Ni and S as compared to the Fe top-site, consistent with the higher adsorption energy over Fe top-site. Also, comparing the ∆ρ when Na atom is placed over top of the Ni and S sites, we found that among the two cases, the charge density is more localized for Ni site. This explains why Ni site has more adsorption energy than S site. Furthermore, we calculated the projected density of states (PDOS) of pure and Na adsorbed hybrid structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The NFCN hybrid shows metallic nature. The contribution to the DOS at the Fermi level comes from metal d- and S p-orbitals. There is no contribution of metal s-orbitals to the DOS at the Fermi level. After Na adsorption, we found that Na DOS is mainly localized above Fermi level at an energy value of 2&nbsp;eV.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e0b83e1b-334f-4f3b-b323-0f9cad3bb6eb/smll202304587-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e0b83e1b-334f-4f3b-b323-0f9cad3bb6eb/smll202304587-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d6c0bc80-8161-445c-8053-e90d5470e2d3/smll202304587-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e0b83e1b-334f-4f3b-b323-0f9cad3bb6eb/smll202304587-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Top and side views of the isosurfaces of differential charge density of Na atom on NFCN hybrid when Na atom is placed on top-sites of the A) Fe, B) Ni, and C) S atoms. The Isosurfaces value is set at 0.0037 e Å<sup>−3</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage performance of the NFCN2 hybrid was investigated in a full cell configuration coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F as a cathode. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304587-fig-0007">7A</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the specific capacity at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a potential range of 1.0–3.5&nbsp;V was detected to be ≈95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles. Initial CE was estimated to be 74%, which subsequently increases up to ≈90% and was maintained for 50 cycles, suggesting good reversibility for NFCN2 hybrid in full cell configuration. In the further cycling for rate capability, the full cell delivers the specific capacities of 140, 93, 53, 35, 23, and 15 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The difference in specific capacity and Coulombic efficiency between half-cell and full-cell configurations can be attributed to significant structural distinctions of these cells. For instance, the half-cell, with sodium metal as a counter electrode, typically exhibits higher electronic conductivity and a thicker SEI layer. Moreover, its larger cell stack leads to lower overall resistance, resulting in a corresponding increase in battery performance compared to the full cell with a cathode electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0041_R_d73807926e1564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Batteries Supercaps">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To estimate contribution of (pseudo)capacitive behavior to the battery performance, the CV curves were measured with scan rates of 0.2–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0007">7C</a>). Based on the equation as aforementioned, the contribution of (pseudo)capacitive behavior appears to be 51% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0007">7D</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304587-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/54c60b90-e1dc-4d2f-9988-3d903144bc24/smll202304587-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/54c60b90-e1dc-4d2f-9988-3d903144bc24/smll202304587-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25002122-dd37-43e8-ae89-dc9e6471a440/smll202304587-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/54c60b90-e1dc-4d2f-9988-3d903144bc24/smll202304587-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304587-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Full cell configuration: A) Schematic illustration of the full cell. B) Cycling behavior and subsequent rate capability, C) CVs within a scan rate of 0.2–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and D) (pseudo)capacitive contribution (green area) to the overall sodium storage of NFCN2 as an anode. In the cycling behavior, initial 5 cycles were measured under 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Nyquist plots of the full cell at E) OCV and F) various potential values. In the C, orange, yellow, green, blue and dark blue represent the scan rates of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8 and 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Inset in the E is a magnified view of the corresponding Nyquist plot at high frequency region. Circles and solid lines represent experimental and fitting results, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>EIS was conducted to determine the alteration in the resistance of the full cell. At OCV, the EIS spectrum displays a semicircle at high frequency range, which is associated with the passivating layer simultaneously formed at the interphase between the electrolyte and electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0042_R_d73807926e1608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochem. Commun.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the initial five cycles that stabilize the passivating SEI layer, the EIS spectra at all potential stages exhibit quite similar aspects with distinct two semicircles at mid and low frequencies (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304587-fig-0007">7E</a>). The semicircles at higher and lower Hz ranges are attributed to the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0043_R_d73807926e1623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ChemElectroChem">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To quantify the resistance values, further simulations were carried out using equivalent circuit including <i>R</i><sub>Sol</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202304587-tbl-0003">3</a>, the alteration in the <i>R</i><sub>Sol</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> appears to be negligible, which could indicate that quite stable passivating layer is formed on the electrode surface. On the other hand, the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> of 45.9 Ω at fully charged stage keeps increasing with discharging processes. At a fully discharged stage, the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> reaches to 150 Ω, which is a three times larger resistance as compared to that at a fully charged stage. Then, the resistance starts to decrease for the following charging process and is totally recovered back to a <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> of 45.3 Ω at the fully charged stage. The observed variation in the R<sub>CT</sub> value is due to the variation in sodiation/desodiation in each stage and clearly demonstrates highly reversible reaction without an increase of resistance for charge-discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0021_R_d73807926e1665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304587-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304587-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we developed a facile synthetic approach to prepare nickel iron bimetallic sulfide incorporated with the CN through a simple chemical transition between Ni and a Fe-containing LDH structure and a single precursor containing C, N, and S atoms. The observed variation in the oxidation states and local distortion of nickel iron sulfide indicate strong electronic coupling at the heterointerface of CN and metal sulfide. Benefiting from the advantages of unique properties of CN and nickel iron sulfides, the NFCN hybrid displays promising SIB performance in both half-cell and full cell configurations with promising cycling stability. Through the DFT calculation, the adsorption energy toward Na<sup>+</sup> ions is calculated to be largest for the Fe atoms in the NFCN hybrid, suggesting the Fe atoms acting as active site. We also demonstrate that the addition of CN in nickel iron sulfide is beneficial in stabilising the passivating SEI layer while excess CN causes slight degradation in the charge transfer property during cycling. Based on the observations on the SIB properties, we believe this research could derive much follow-up research to fully explore the potential of the CN framework with different metal sulfide systems for applications in energy storage and conversion and beyond.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304587-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304587-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202304587-sec-0070-title"> Sample Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Ni─Fe LDH precursor was prepared as per the typical coprecipitation method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0044_R_d73807926e1857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Phys. Chem. C">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The molar ratio of Ni and Fe was set to 2. The NFCN hybrid was synthesized through a calcination of evenly mixed DTO (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>) and Ni─Fe LDH precursors at 700&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Three samples were prepared with different DTO and Ni─Fe LDH weight ratios of 2:1, 6:1 and 8:1 (600&nbsp;mg in total in one batch) and are denoted as NFCN1, NFCN2 and NFCN3, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202304587-sec-0080-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed by using a PANalytical X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα (<i>λ</i> = 0.1548&nbsp;nm) radiation. The 2<i>θ</i> range for the recorded diffraction peaks is from 5.0° and 80° and a step size of 0.014 per second. Morphological evolution was observed with field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Hitachi SU8230) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM, JEOL FEM 7001) with the accelerating voltage electron beam of 200&nbsp;kV. The near-edge X-ray absorption fine structure (NEXAFS) spectra were obtained on the Soft X-ray Spectroscopy Beamline at the Singapore Synchrotron facility, Singapore. All spectra were recorded in total electron yield (TEY) mode to enhance the surface resolution, and the sample signals were then double normalized to the incidence intensity of the photon beam measured with a gold mesh. X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurement was carried out at the XAS beamline of the Australian Synchrotron, Australia's Nuclear Science and Technology Organization (ANSTO) in Australia. To obtain X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) data, the samples were measured in room temperature chamber in a transmission mode.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202304587-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The half-cell configuration was used to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage behavior of the NFCN hybrids. For the electrochemical analysis, the anode electrode was fabricated by mixing with 80&nbsp;wt% of active material, 10&nbsp;wt% of acetylene black and 10&nbsp;wt% of mixture binder of polyacrylic acid and carboxymethyl cellulose. The resultant slurry was spread onto the copper foil and dried at 100 °C in the vacuum oven overnight. Each electrode area was 1.539 cm<sup>2</sup>, whereas the mass of active materials loaded onto the copper foil was about 1.5−2.0&nbsp;mg or 0.974−1.299&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The battery cell was assembled using the 2032 standard coin cell in an Argon filled glove box. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC):dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1 by volume) was used as an electrolyte for SIB whereas a thin polypropylene membrane was used as a separator. All the electrochemical experiments were performed in a galvanostatic mode with WonATech multichannel battery cycler in the voltage range of 0.01−3.0&nbsp;V. For full-cell configuration, the NFCN2 hybrid and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F were used as an anode and a cathode, respectively. The cathode slurry was fabricated with 90&nbsp;wt% of active material, 5&nbsp;wt% of the acetylene black and 5&nbsp;wt% of the mixture binder of polyacrylic acid and carboxymethyl cellulose. The weight ratio of anode and cathode was set to around 1:1.5. The evolution of the resistances of not only the half cell with NFCN hybrids but also the full cell with NFCN2 was investigated by carrying out the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis using WonATech impedance analyser in the frequency region of 0.01−10<sup>5</sup> Hz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202304587-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202304587-sec-0100-title"> Theoretical Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the calculations were performed using density functional theory (DFT)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0045_R_d73807926e1927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Phys. Rev.">45</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0046_R_d73807926e1927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> formalism as implemented in the Vienna Ab-initio Simulation Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0047_R_d73807926e1932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Phys. Rev. B">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The projector augmented wave (PAW)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0004_R_d73807926e1937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Rev. B">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> method along with the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0048_R_d73807926e1943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Phys. Rev. B">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> parameterization within the framework of generalized gradient approximation (GGA)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0049_R_d73807926e1948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to describe the electronic exchange-correlation effects. A converged kinetic energy cut-off of 450&nbsp;eV was employed for the expansion of plane waves, and a Gamma centered mesh of 2 × 2 × 1 was used for geometry optimization. The energy convergence criterion of 10–5&nbsp;eV was set for the electronic convergence and 0.01&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup> for geometry optimization. For self-consistent calculations, a gamma-centered mesh of 4 × 4 × 1 was used. The Van-der-Waals dispersive interactions were included using D3 approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0050_R_d73807926e1956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Chem. Phys.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To model the CN (C<sub>3</sub>N) and nickel iron sulfide heterostructure, a surface along (111) for the nickel iron sulfide crystal structure was employed. The (111) surface was used to assess the Na adsorption as the surface is energetically more stable than (100) and (110) surfaces of the nickel iron sulfide structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304587-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0051_R_d73807926e1963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Phys. Chem. C">51</a>, <a href="#smll202304587-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304587-bib-0052_R_d73807926e1963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lattice mismatch was less than 1%. The heterostructure contains a total of 208 atoms (72 of CN and 136 of nickel iron sulfide (111) surface).</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="smll202304587-bib-0004"><span class="bullet">4</span><span class="author">J. J. Mortensen</span>, <span class="author">L. B. Hansen</span>, <span class="author">K.
